Wells</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Science fiction writer from England</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/H._G._Wells_(disambiguation)" title="H. P. Wells">George Phillip "G. P." Wells</a> (1901&#8211;1985)<br/>Frank Richard Wells (1903&#8211;1982)<br/>Anna-Jane Kennard (1909&#8211;2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>)<br/><a href="/wiki/Anthony_West_(author)" title="Anthony West (author)">Anthony West</a> (1914&#8211;1987)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Relatives</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;"><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wells_(cricketer)" title="Joseph Wells (cricketer)">Joseph Wells</a> (father)<br/>Sarah Neal (mother)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Herbert George Wells</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFE_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFE-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parrinder_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parrinder-4">[4]</a></sup> (21 September 1866 &#8211; 13 August 1946) was an English writer. He was prolific in many genres, writing dozens of novels, short stories, and works of social commentary, satire, biography, and autobiography, and even including two books on recreational war games. He is now best remembered for his <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> novels and is often called a "father of science fiction", along with <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Gernsback" title="Hugo Gernsback">Hugo Gernsback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfhof_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfhof-7">[a]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his own lifetime, however, he was most prominent as a forward-looking, even prophetic social critic who devoted his literary talents to the development of a progressive vision on a global scale. A <a href="/wiki/Futurist" title="Futurist">futurist</a>, he wrote a number of utopian works and foresaw the advent of aircraft, tanks, space travel, nuclear weapons, satellite television and something resembling the World Wide Web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> His science fiction imagined time travel, alien invasion, invisibility, and biological engineering. <a href="/wiki/Brian_Aldiss" title="Brian Aldiss">Brian Aldiss</a> referred to Wells as the "Shakespeare of science fiction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> His most notable science fiction works include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i> (1895), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau" title="The Island of Doctor Moreau">The Island of Doctor Moreau</a></i> (1896), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Invisible_Man" title="The Invisible Man">The Invisible Man</a></i> (1897), <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> (1898) and the military science fiction <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_in_the_Air" title="The War in the Air">The War in the Air</a></i> (1907). Wells was nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> four times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_nominations_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_nominations-10">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wells\'s earliest specialised training was in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and his thinking on ethical matters took place in a specifically and fundamentally <a href="/wiki/Darwinism" title="Darwinism">Darwinian</a> context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> He was also from an early date an outspoken <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a>, often (but not always, as at the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>) sympathising with <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifist</a> views. His later works became increasingly political and <a href="/wiki/Didacticism" title="Didacticism">didactic</a>, and he wrote little science fiction, while he sometimes indicated on official documents that his profession was that of journalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Novels such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kipps" title="Kipps">Kipps</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Mr_Polly" title="The History of Mr Polly">The History of Mr Polly</a></i>, which describe lower-middle-class life, led to the suggestion that he was a worthy successor to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> but Wells described a range of social strata and even attempted, in <i><a href="/wiki/Tono-Bungay" title="Tono-Bungay">Tono-Bungay</a></i> (1909), a diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/English_society" title="English society">English society</a> as a whole. A diabetic, Wells co-founded the charity The Diabetic Association (known today as <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_UK" title="Diabetes UK">Diabetes UK</a>) in 1934.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Herbert George Wells was born at Atlas House, 162 High Street in <a href="/wiki/Bromley" title="Bromley">Bromley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-14">[13]</a></sup> on 21 September 1866.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parrinder_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parrinder-4">[4]</a></sup> Called "Bertie" in the family, he was the fourth and last child of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wells_(cricketer)" title="Joseph Wells (cricketer)">Joseph Wells</a> (a former domestic gardener, and at the time a shopkeeper and professional <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricketer</a>) and his wife, Sarah Neal (a former <a href="/wiki/Domestic_worker" title="Domestic worker">domestic servant</a>). An inheritance had allowed the family to acquire a shop in which they sold china and sporting goods, although it failed to prosper: the stock was old and worn out, and the location was poor. Joseph Wells managed to earn a meagre income, but little of it came from the shop and he received an unsteady amount of money from playing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amateur_and_professional_cricketers" title="Amateur and professional cricketers">professional cricket</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Kent_County_Cricket_Club" title="Kent County Cricket Club">Kent county team</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-15">[14]</a></sup> Payment for skilled bowlers and batsmen came from voluntary donations afterwards, or from small payments from the clubs where matches were played.\n</p><p>A defining incident of young Wells\'s life was an accident in 1874 that left him bedridden with a broken leg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parrinder_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Parrinder-4">[4]</a></sup> To pass the time he began to read books from the local library, brought to him by his father. He soon became devoted to the other worlds and lives to which books gave him access; they also stimulated his desire to write. Later that year he entered Thomas Morley\'s Commercial Academy, a <a href="/wiki/Private_school" title="Private school">private school</a> founded in 1849, following the bankruptcy of Morley\'s earlier school. The teaching was erratic, the curriculum mostly focused, Wells later said, on producing <a href="/wiki/Copperplate_script" title="Copperplate script">copperplate handwriting</a> and doing the sort of sums useful to tradesmen. Wells continued at Morley\'s Academy until 1880. In 1877, his father, Joseph Wells, suffered a fractured thigh. The accident effectively put an end to Joseph\'s career as a cricketer, and his subsequent earnings as a shopkeeper were not enough to compensate for the loss of the primary source of family income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1840" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg/220px-Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg/330px-Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg/440px-Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uppark-Sfront-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wells spent the winter of 1887/88 convalescing at <a href="/wiki/Uppark" title="Uppark">Uppark</a>, where his mother, Sarah, was housekeeper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pevsner_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner-17">[16]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>No longer able to support themselves financially, the family instead sought to place their sons as <a href="/wiki/Apprenticeship" title="Apprenticeship">apprentices</a> in various occupations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> From 1880 to 1883, Wells had an unhappy apprenticeship as a <a href="/wiki/Draper" title="Draper">draper</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Southsea" title="Southsea">Southsea</a> Drapery Emporium, Hyde\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHW1925_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-GHW1925-19">[18]</a></sup> His experiences at Hyde\'s, where he worked a thirteen-hour day and slept in a dormitory with other apprentices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-14">[13]</a></sup> later inspired his novels <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wheels_of_Chance" title="The Wheels of Chance">The Wheels of Chance</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Mr_Polly" title="The History of Mr Polly">The History of Mr Polly</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Kipps" title="Kipps">Kipps</a></i>, which portray the life of a draper\'s apprentice as well as providing a critique of society\'s distribution of wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wells\'s parents had a turbulent marriage, owing primarily to his mother\'s being a <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> and his father\'s being a <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethinker</a>. When his mother returned to work as a lady\'s maid (at <a href="/wiki/Uppark" title="Uppark">Uppark</a>, a <a href="/wiki/English_country_house" title="English country house">country house</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a>), one of the conditions of work was that she would not be permitted to have living space for her husband and children. Thereafter, she and Joseph lived separate lives, though they never divorced and remained faithful to each other. As a consequence, Herbert\'s personal troubles increased as he subsequently failed as a draper and also, later, as a chemist\'s assistant. However, Uppark had a magnificent library in which he immersed himself, reading many classic works, including <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Utopia_(book)" title="Utopia (book)">Utopia</a></i>, and the works of <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFarland_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-McFarland-21">[20]</a></sup> This would be the beginning of Wells\'s venture into literature.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teacher">Teacher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Teacher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells,_c.1890.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/H._G._Wells%2C_c.1890.jpg/220px-H._G._Wells%2C_c.1890.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/H._G._Wells%2C_c.1890.jpg/330px-H._G._Wells%2C_c.1890.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/H._G._Wells%2C_c.1890.jpg/440px-H._G._Wells%2C_c.1890.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells,_c.1890.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wells studying in London <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1890</div></div></div>\n<p>In October 1879, Wells\'s mother arranged through a distant relative, Arthur Williams, for him to join the <a href="/wiki/National_school_(England_and_Wales)" title="National school (England and Wales)">National School</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wookey" title="Wookey">Wookey</a> in Somerset as a pupil&#8211;teacher, a senior pupil who acted as a teacher of younger children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHW1925_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-GHW1925-19">[18]</a></sup> In December that year, however, Williams was dismissed for irregularities in his qualifications and Wells was returned to Uppark. After a short apprenticeship at a chemist in nearby <a href="/wiki/Midhurst" title="Midhurst">Midhurst</a> and an even shorter stay as a boarder at <a href="/wiki/Midhurst_Grammar_School" title="Midhurst Grammar School">Midhurst Grammar School</a>, he signed his apprenticeship papers at Hyde\'s. In 1883, Wells persuaded his parents to release him from the apprenticeship, taking an opportunity offered by Midhurst Grammar School again to become a pupil&#8211;teacher; his proficiency in Latin and science during his earlier short stay had been remembered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHW1925_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-GHW1925-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The years he spent in Southsea had been the most miserable of his life to that point, but his good fortune at securing a position at Midhurst Grammar School meant that Wells could continue his self-education in earnest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-15">[14]</a></sup> The following year, Wells won a scholarship to the Normal School of Science (later the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Science" title="Royal College of Science">Royal College of Science</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Kensington" title="South Kensington">South Kensington</a>, now part of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_College_London" title="Imperial College London">Imperial College London</a>) in London, studying <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> under <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_school_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wells_school-22">[21]</a></sup> As an alumnus, he later helped to set up the Royal College of Science Association, of which he became the first president in 1909. Wells studied in his new school until 1887, with a weekly allowance of 21 <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" title="Guinea (British coin)">guinea</a>) thanks to his scholarship. This ought to have been a comfortable sum of money (at the time many <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> families had "round about a pound a week" as their entire household income)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> yet in his <i>Experiment in Autobiography</i>, Wells speaks of constantly being hungry, and indeed photographs of him at the time show a youth who is very thin and malnourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="295" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png/170px-H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png/255px-H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_-_Sandgate_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13715.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>H. G. Wells in 1907 at the door of his house at <a href="/wiki/Sandgate,_Kent" title="Sandgate, Kent">Sandgate</a></div></div></div>\n<p>He soon entered the Debating Society of the school. These years mark the beginning of his interest in a possible reformation of society. At first approaching the subject through Plato\'s <i>Republic</i>, he soon turned to contemporary ideas of socialism as expressed by the recently formed <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a> and free lectures delivered at <a href="/wiki/Kelmscott_House" title="Kelmscott House">Kelmscott House</a>, the home of <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a>. He was also among the founders of <i>The Science School Journal</i>, a school magazine that allowed him to express his views on literature and society, as well as trying his hand at fiction; a precursor to his novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i> was published in the journal under the title <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronic_Argonauts" title="The Chronic Argonauts">The Chronic Argonauts</a></i>. The school year 1886&#8211;87 was the last year of his studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_school_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wells_school-22">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 1888, Wells stayed in <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a>, living in <a href="/wiki/Basford,_Staffordshire" title="Basford, Staffordshire">Basford</a>. The unique environment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Potteries" title="The Potteries">The Potteries</a> was certainly an inspiration. He wrote in a letter to a friend from the area that "the district made an immense impression on me." The inspiration for some of his descriptions in <i>The War of the Worlds</i> is thought to have come from his short time spent here, seeing the iron foundry furnaces burn over the city, shooting huge red light into the skies. His stay in The Potteries also resulted in the macabre short story "<a href="/wiki/The_Cone" title="The Cone">The Cone</a>" (1895, contemporaneous with his famous <i>The Time Machine</i>), set in the north of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After teaching for some time, he was briefly on the staff of <a href="/wiki/Holt,_Wrexham_County_Borough" title="Holt, Wrexham County Borough">Holt</a> Academy in Wales<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> &#8211; Wells found it necessary to supplement his knowledge relating to educational principles and methodology and entered the College of Preceptors (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_of_Teachers" title="College of Teachers">College of Teachers</a>). He later received his Licentiate and Fellowship <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_of_Teachers" title="College of Teachers">FCP</a> diplomas from the College. It was not until 1890 that Wells earned a Bachelor of Science degree in <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_London_External_System" title="University of London External System">University of London External Programme</a>. In 1889&#8211;90, he managed to find a post as a teacher at Henley House School, where he taught <a href="/wiki/A._A._Milne" title="A. A. Milne">A. A. Milne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> His first published work was a <i>Text-Book of Biology</i> in two volumes (1893).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon leaving the Normal School of Science, Wells was left without a source of income. His aunt Mary&#8212;his father\'s sister-in-law&#8212;invited him to stay with her for a while, which solved his immediate problem of accommodation. During his stay at his aunt\'s residence, he grew increasingly interested in her daughter, Isabel. He would later go on to court her. To earn money, he began writing short humorous articles for journals such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pall_Mall_Gazette" title="The Pall Mall Gazette">The Pall Mall Gazette</a></i>, later collecting these in volume form as <i><a href="/wiki/Select_Conversations_with_an_Uncle" title="Select Conversations with an Uncle">Select Conversations with an Uncle</a></i> (1895) and <i><a href="/wiki/Certain_Personal_Matters" title="Certain Personal Matters">Certain Personal Matters</a></i> (1897). So prolific did Wells become at this mode of journalism that many of his early pieces remain unidentified. According to David C Smith, "Most of Wells\'s occasional pieces have not been collected, and many have not even been identified as his. Wells did not automatically receive the byline his reputation demanded until after 1896 or so ... As a result, many of his early pieces are unknown. It is obvious that many early Wells items have been lost."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> His success with these shorter pieces encouraged him to write book-length work, and he published his first novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i>, in 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="361" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg/170px-MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg/255px-MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg/340px-MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MayburyRoad_h.g.wells_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>141 Maybury Rd, <a href="/wiki/Woking" title="Woking">Woking</a>, where Wells lived from May 1895 until late 1896<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-celebWoking-HGW_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-celebWoking-HGW-32">[31]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1891, Wells <a href="/wiki/Cousin_marriage" title="Cousin marriage">married his cousin</a> Isabel Mary Wells. The couple agreed to separate in 1894 when he fell in love with one of his students, Amy Catherine Robbins (later known as Jane), with whom he moved to <a href="/wiki/Woking" title="Woking">Woking</a>, Surrey in May 1895. They lived in a rented house, \'Lynton\', (now No.141) Maybury Road in the town centre for just under 18 months<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIW-WBC_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-WIW-WBC-33">[32]</a></sup> and married at St Pancras register office in October 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> His short period in Woking was perhaps the most creative and productive of his whole writing career,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIW-WBC_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-WIW-WBC-33">[32]</a></sup> for while there he planned and wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i>, completed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau" title="The Island of Doctor Moreau">The Island of Doctor Moreau</a></i>, wrote and published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wonderful_Visit" title="The Wonderful Visit">The Wonderful Visit</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wheels_of_Chance" title="The Wheels of Chance">The Wheels of Chance</a></i>, and began writing two other early books, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/When_the_Sleeper_Wakes" title="When the Sleeper Wakes">When the Sleeper Wakes</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Love_and_Mr_Lewisham" title="Love and Mr Lewisham">Love and Mr Lewisham</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIW-WBC_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-WIW-WBC-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoogleRiddle_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoogleRiddle-35">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late summer 1896, Wells and Jane moved to a larger house in <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Park" title="Worcester Park">Worcester Park</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston upon Thames</a>, for two years; this lasted until his poor health took them to Sandgate, near <a href="/wiki/Folkestone" title="Folkestone">Folkestone</a>, where he constructed a large family home, <a href="/wiki/Spade_House" title="Spade House">Spade House</a>, in 1901. He had two sons with Jane: <a href="/wiki/G._P._Wells" title="G. P. Wells">George Philip</a> (known as "Gip"; 1901&#8211;1985) and Frank Richard (1903&#8211;1982).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With his wife Jane\'s consent, Wells had <a href="/wiki/Affair" title="Affair">affairs</a> with a number of women, including the American <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> activist <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Sanger" title="Margaret Sanger">Margaret Sanger</a>, adventurer and writer <a href="/wiki/Odette_Keun" title="Odette Keun">Odette Keun</a>, Soviet spy <a href="/wiki/Moura_Budberg" title="Moura Budberg">Moura Budberg</a> and novelist <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_von_Arnim" title="Elizabeth von Arnim">Elizabeth von Arnim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> In 1909, he had a daughter, Anna-Jane, with the writer <a href="/wiki/Amber_Reeves" title="Amber Reeves">Amber Reeves</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> whose parents, <a href="/wiki/William_Pember_Reeves" title="William Pember Reeves">William</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maud_Pember_Reeves" title="Maud Pember Reeves">Maud Pember Reeves</a>, he had met through the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a>; and in 1914, a son, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_West_(author)" title="Anthony West (author)">Anthony West</a> (1914&#8211;1987), by the novelist and <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminist</a> <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_West" title="Rebecca West">Rebecca West</a>, 26 years his junior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Webb" title="Beatrice Webb">Beatrice Webb</a> voiced disapproval of Wells\' "sordid intrigue" with the daughter of veteran Fabian <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Olivier,_1st_Baron_Olivier" title="Sydney Olivier, 1st Baron Olivier">Sydney Olivier</a>, he responded by lampooning Beatrice Webb and her husband Sidney Webb in his 1911 novel <i>The New Machiavelli</i> as \'Altiora and Oscar Bailey\', a pair of short-sighted, bourgeois manipulators. In <i>Experiment in Autobiography</i> (1934), Wells wrote: "I was never a great amorist, though I have loved several people very deeply".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/David_Lodge_(author)" title="David Lodge (author)">David Lodge</a>\'s novel <i>A Man of Parts</i> (2011)&#8212;a \'narrative based on factual sources\' (author\'s note)&#8212;gives a convincing and generally sympathetic account of Wells\'s relations with the women mentioned above, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Director <a href="/wiki/Simon_Wells" title="Simon Wells">Simon Wells</a> (born 1961), the author\'s great-grandson, was a consultant on the future scenes in <i><a href="/wiki/Back_to_the_Future_Part_II" title="Back to the Future Part II">Back to the Future Part II</a></i> (1989).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artist">Artist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Artist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>One of the ways that Wells expressed himself was through his drawings and sketches. One common location for these was the endpapers and title pages of his own diaries, and they covered a wide variety of topics, from political commentary to his feelings toward his literary contemporaries and his current romantic interests. During his marriage to Amy Catherine, whom he nicknamed Jane, he drew a considerable number of pictures, many of them being overt comments on their marriage. During this period, he called these pictures "picshuas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> These picshuas have been the topic of study by Wells scholars for many years, and in 2006, a book was published on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writer">Writer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Writer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woking_tripod.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Woking_tripod.JPG/170px-Woking_tripod.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Woking_tripod.JPG/255px-Woking_tripod.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Woking_tripod.JPG/340px-Woking_tripod.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woking_tripod.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tripod_(The_War_of_the_Worlds)" title="Tripod (The War of the Worlds)">tripod</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Woking" title="Woking">Woking</a>, England. The book is a seminal depiction of a conflict between mankind and an <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrials_in_fiction" title="Extraterrestrials in fiction">extraterrestrial</a> race.</div></div></div>\n<p>Some of his early novels, called "<a href="/wiki/Scientific_romance" title="Scientific romance">scientific romances</a>", invented several themes now classic in science fiction in such works as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau" title="The Island of Doctor Moreau">The Island of Doctor Moreau</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Invisible_Man" title="The Invisible Man">The Invisible Man</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sleeper_Awakes" title="The Sleeper Awakes">When the Sleeper Wakes</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_First_Men_in_the_Moon" title="The First Men in the Moon">The First Men in the Moon</a></i>. He also wrote realistic novels that received critical acclaim, including <i><a href="/wiki/Kipps" title="Kipps">Kipps</a></i> and a critique of English culture during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwardian_period" title="Edwardian period">Edwardian period</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tono-Bungay" title="Tono-Bungay">Tono-Bungay</a></i>. Wells also wrote dozens of short stories and novellas, including, "The Flowering of the Strange Orchid", which helped bring the full impact of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwin</a>\'s revolutionary botanical ideas to a wider public, and was followed by many later successes such as "<a href="/wiki/The_Country_of_the_Blind" title="The Country of the Blind">The Country of the Blind</a>" (1904).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/James_E._Gunn_(writer)" title="James E. Gunn (writer)">James Gunn</a>, one of Wells\'s major contributions to the science fiction genre was his approach, which he referred to as his "new system of ideas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> In his opinion, the author should always strive to make the story as credible as possible, even if both the writer and the reader knew certain elements are impossible, allowing the reader to accept the ideas as something that could really happen, today referred to as "the plausible impossible" and "<a href="/wiki/Suspension_of_disbelief" title="Suspension of disbelief">suspension of disbelief</a>". While neither invisibility nor time travel was new in speculative fiction, Wells added a sense of realism to the concepts which the readers were not familiar with. He conceived the idea of using a vehicle that allows an operator to travel purposely and selectively forwards or backwards in time. The term "time machine", coined by Wells, is now almost universally used to refer to such a vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFarland_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-McFarland-21">[20]</a></sup> He explained that while writing <i>The Time Machine</i>, he realized that "the more impossible the story I had to tell, the more ordinary must be the setting, and the circumstances in which I now set the <a href="/wiki/Time_travel" title="Time travel">Time Traveller</a> were all that I could imagine of solid upper-class comforts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> In "Wells\'s Law", a science fiction story should contain only a single extraordinary assumption. Being aware the notion of magic as something real had disappeared from society, he, therefore, used scientific ideas and theories as a substitute for magic to justify the impossible. Wells\'s best-known statement of the "law" appears in his introduction to <i>The Scientific Romances of H. G. Wells</i> (1933),\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>As soon as the magic trick has been done the whole business of the fantasy writer is to keep everything else human and real. Touches of prosaic detail are imperative and a rigorous adherence to the hypothesis. Any extra fantasy outside the cardinal assumption immediately gives a touch of irresponsible silliness to the invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Griffin_(The_Invisible_Man)" title="Griffin (The Invisible Man)">Dr. Griffin / The Invisible Man</a> is a brilliant research scientist who discovers a method of invisibility, but finds himself unable to reverse the process. An enthusiast of random and irresponsible violence, Griffin has become an iconic character in <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror fiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> sees a shipwrecked man left on the island home of Doctor Moreau, a <a href="/wiki/Mad_scientist" title="Mad scientist">mad scientist</a> who creates <a href="/wiki/Human%E2%80%93animal_hybrid" title="Human&#8211;animal hybrid">human-like hybrid beings</a> from animals via vivisection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> The earliest depiction of <a href="/wiki/Uplift_(science_fiction)" title="Uplift (science fiction)">uplift</a>, the novel deals with a number of philosophical themes, including pain and cruelty, moral responsibility, human identity, and human interference with nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Though <i>Tono-Bungay</i> is not a science-fiction novel, radioactive decay plays a small but consequential role in it. Radioactive decay plays a much larger role in <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Set_Free" title="The World Set Free">The World Set Free</a></i> (1914). This book contains what is surely his biggest prophetic "hit", with the first description of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuclear_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear-52">[51]</a></sup> Scientists of the day were well aware that the natural decay of <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> releases energy at a slow rate over thousands of years. The <i>rate</i> of release is too slow to have practical utility, but the <i>total amount</i> released is huge. Wells\'s novel revolves around an (unspecified) invention that accelerates the process of radioactive decay, producing bombs that explode with no more than the force of ordinary high explosives&#8212;but which "continue to explode" for days on end. "Nothing could have been more obvious to the people of the earlier twentieth century", he wrote, "than the rapidity with which war was becoming impossible&#160;... [but] they did not see it until the atomic bombs burst in their fumbling hands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuclear_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear-52">[51]</a></sup> In 1932, the physicist and conceiver of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" title="Le&#243; Szil&#225;rd">Le&#243; Szil&#225;rd</a> read <i>The World Set Free</i> (the same year Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>), a book which he said made a great impression on him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg/220px-H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg/330px-H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg/440px-H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_crater_5163_med.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells_(crater)" title="H. G. Wells (crater)">H. G. Wells crater</a>, located on the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a>, was named after the author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_First_Men_in_the_Moon" title="The First Men in the Moon">The First Men in the Moon</a></i> (1901) in 1970.</div></div></div>\n<p>Wells also wrote non-fiction. His first non-fiction <a href="/wiki/Bestseller" title="Bestseller">bestseller</a> was <i><a href="/wiki/Anticipations" title="Anticipations">Anticipations of the Reaction of Mechanical and Scientific Progress upon Human Life and Thought</a></i> (1901). When originally serialised in a magazine it was subtitled "An Experiment in Prophecy", and is considered his most explicitly <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">futuristic</a> work. It offered the immediate political message of the privileged sections of society continuing to bar capable men from other classes from advancement until war would force a need to employ those most able, rather than the traditional upper classes, as leaders. Anticipating what the world would be like in the year 2000, the book is interesting both for its hits (trains and cars resulting in the dispersion of populations from cities to suburbs; moral restrictions declining as men and women seek greater sexual freedom; the defeat of German <a href="/wiki/Militarism" title="Militarism">militarism</a>, and the existence of a European Union) and its misses (he did not expect successful <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> before 1950, and averred that "my imagination refuses to see any sort of submarine doing anything but suffocate its crew and founder at sea").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>His bestselling two-volume work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Outline_of_History" title="The Outline of History">The Outline of History</a></i> (1920), began a new era of popularised world history. It received a mixed critical response from professional historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> However, it was very popular amongst the general population and made Wells a rich man. Many other authors followed with "Outlines" of their own in other subjects. He reprised his <i>Outline</i> in 1922 with a much shorter popular work, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Short_History_of_the_World_(H._G._Wells)" title="A Short History of the World (H. G. Wells)">A Short History of the World</a></i>, a history book praised by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> and two long efforts, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Science_of_Life" title="The Science of Life">The Science of Life</a></i> (1930) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Work,_Wealth_and_Happiness_of_Mankind" title="The Work, Wealth and Happiness of Mankind">The Work, Wealth and Happiness of Mankind</a></i> (1931).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> The "Outlines" became sufficiently common for <a href="/wiki/James_Thurber" title="James Thurber">James Thurber</a> to parody the trend in his humorous essay, "An Outline of Scientists"&#8212;indeed, Wells\'s <i>Outline of History</i> remains in print with a new 2005 edition, while <i>A Short History of the World</i> has been re-edited (2006).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="1367" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg/170px-H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg/255px-H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg/340px-H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells_Daily_Mirror.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>H. G. Wells <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1918</div></div></div>\n<p>From quite early in Wells\'s career, he sought a better way to organise society and wrote a number of <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">Utopian</a> novels. The first of these was <i><a href="/wiki/A_Modern_Utopia" title="A Modern Utopia">A Modern Utopia</a></i> (1905), which shows a worldwide utopia with "no imports but meteorites, and no exports at all";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> two travellers from our world fall into its <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">alternate history</a>. The others usually begin with the world rushing to catastrophe, until people realise a better way of living: whether by mysterious gases from a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> causing people to behave rationally and abandoning a European war (<i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Days_of_the_Comet" title="In the Days of the Comet">In the Days of the Comet</a></i> (1906)), or a world council of scientists taking over, as in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Shape_of_Things_to_Come" title="The Shape of Things to Come">The Shape of Things to Come</a></i> (1933, which he later adapted for the 1936 <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Korda" title="Alexander Korda">Alexander Korda</a> film, <i><a href="/wiki/Things_to_Come" title="Things to Come">Things to Come</a></i>). This depicted, all too accurately, the impending <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War</a>, with cities being destroyed by aerial bombs. He also portrayed the rise of <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascist</a> dictators in <i>The Autocracy of Mr Parham</i> (1930) and <i>The Holy Terror</i> (1939). <i><a href="/wiki/Men_Like_Gods" title="Men Like Gods">Men Like Gods</a></i> (1923) is also a utopian novel. Wells in this period was regarded as an enormously influential figure; the critic <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Cowley" title="Malcolm Cowley">Malcolm Cowley</a> stated: "by the time he was forty, his influence was wider than any other living English writer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wells contemplates the ideas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_and_nurture" title="Nature and nurture">nature and nurture</a> and questions humanity in books such as <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i>. Not all his scientific romances ended in a Utopia, and Wells also wrote a <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopian</a> novel, <i>When the Sleeper Wakes</i> (1899, rewritten as <i>The Sleeper Awakes</i>, 1910), which pictures a future society where the classes have become more and more separated, leading to a revolt of the masses against the rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> is even darker. The narrator, having been trapped on an island of animals vivisected (unsuccessfully) into human beings, eventually returns to England; like <a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver\'s Travels">Gulliver</a> on his return from the <a href="/wiki/Houyhnhnm" title="Houyhnhnm">Houyhnhnms</a>, he finds himself unable to shake off the perceptions of his fellow humans as barely civilised beasts, slowly reverting to their animal natures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moreau_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moreau-64">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wells also wrote the preface for the first edition of <a href="/wiki/W._N._P._Barbellion" title="W. N. P. Barbellion">W. N. P. Barbellion</a>\'s diaries, <i>The Journal of a Disappointed Man</i>, published in 1919. Since "Barbellion" was the real author\'s <a href="/wiki/Pen_name" title="Pen name">pen name</a>, many reviewers believed Wells to have been the true author of the <i>Journal</i>; Wells always denied this, despite being full of praise for the diaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg/220px-H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg/330px-H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells-TIME-1926.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wells on the front cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine, 20 September 1926</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1927, a Canadian teacher and writer <a href="/wiki/Florence_Deeks" title="Florence Deeks">Florence Deeks</a> unsuccessfully sued Wells for infringement of copyright and breach of trust, claiming that much of <i>The Outline of History</i> had been plagiarised from her unpublished manuscript,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> <i>The Web of the World\'s Romance</i>, which had spent nearly nine months in the hands of Wells\'s Canadian publisher, Macmillan Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> However, it was sworn on oath at the trial that the manuscript remained in Toronto in the safekeeping of Macmillan, and that Wells did not even know it existed, let alone had seen it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> The court found no proof of copying, and decided the similarities were due to the fact that the books had similar nature and both writers had access to the same sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlorence_A._Deeks_v_H.G._Wells..._Supreme_Court9_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlorence_A._Deeks_v_H.G._Wells..._Supreme_Court9-69">[68]</a></sup> In 2000, <a href="/wiki/A._B._McKillop" title="A. B. McKillop">A. B. McKillop</a>, a professor of history at Carleton University, produced a book on the case, <i>The Spinster &amp; The Prophet: Florence Deeks, H. G. Wells, and the Mystery of the Purloined Past</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> According to McKillop, the lawsuit was unsuccessful due to the prejudice against a woman suing a well-known and famous male author, and he paints a detailed story based on the circumstantial evidence of the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> In 2004, Denis N. Magnusson, Professor Emeritus of the Faculty of Law, Queen\'s University, Ontario, published an article on <i>Deeks v. Wells</i>. This re-examines the case in relation to McKillop\'s book. While having some sympathy for Deeks, he argues that she had a weak case that was not well presented, and though she may have met with <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexism</a> from her lawyers, she received a fair trial, adding that the law applied is essentially the same law that would be applied to a similar case today (i.e., 2004).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1933, Wells predicted in <i>The Shape of Things to Come</i> that the world war he feared would begin in January 1940,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> a prediction which ultimately came true four months early, in September 1939, with the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagar2004_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wagar2004-74">[73]</a></sup> In 1936, before the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a>, Wells called for the compilation of a constantly growing and changing World <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia" title="Encyclopaedia">Encyclopaedia</a>, to be reviewed by outstanding authorities and made accessible to every human being. In 1938, he published a collection of essays on the future organisation of knowledge and education, <i><a href="/wiki/World_Brain" title="World Brain">World Brain</a></i>, including the essay "The Idea of a Permanent World Encyclopaedia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells_(5026568202).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1617" data-file-width="1617" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/H._G._Wells_%285026568202%29.jpg/220px-H._G._Wells_%285026568202%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/H._G._Wells_%285026568202%29.jpg/330px-H._G._Wells_%285026568202%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/H._G._Wells_%285026568202%29.jpg/440px-H._G._Wells_%285026568202%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells_(5026568202).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plaque by the <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells_Society" title="H. G. Wells Society">H. G. Wells Society</a> at Chiltern Court, Baker Street in the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">City of Westminster</a>, London, where Wells lived between 1930 and 1936</div></div></div>\n<p>Prior to 1933, Wells\'s books were widely read in Germany and Austria, and most of his science fiction works had been translated shortly after publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partington-76">[75]</a></sup> By 1933, he had attracted the attention of German officials because of his criticism of the political situation in Germany, and on 10 May 1933, Wells\'s books were <a href=\'/wiki/List_of_book-burning_incidents#Jewish,_anti-Nazi_and_"degenerate"_books_(by_the_Nazis)\' title="List of book-burning incidents">burned by the Nazi youth</a> in Berlin\'s <a href="/wiki/Bebelplatz" title="Bebelplatz">Opernplatz</a>, and his works were banned from libraries and book stores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Partington-76">[75]</a></sup> Wells, as president of <a href="/wiki/PEN_International" title="PEN International">PEN International</a> (Poets, Essayists, Novelists), angered the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazis</a> by overseeing the expulsion of the German PEN club from the international body in 1934 following the German PEN\'s refusal to admit non-<a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">Aryan</a> writers to its membership. At a PEN conference in <a href="/wiki/Dubrovnik" title="Dubrovnik">Ragusa</a>, Wells refused to yield to Nazi sympathisers who demanded that the exiled author <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Toller" title="Ernst Toller">Ernst Toller</a> be prevented from speaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Partington-76">[75]</a></sup> Near the end of the World War II, <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied forces</a> discovered that the <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">SS</a> had compiled lists of people slated for immediate arrest during the invasion of Britain in the abandoned <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a>, with Wells included in the alphabetical list of "<a href="/wiki/The_Black_Book" title="The Black Book">The Black Book</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Seeking a more structured way to play war games, Wells also wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Floor_Games" title="Floor Games">Floor Games</a></i> (1911) followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Wars" title="Little Wars">Little Wars</a></i> (1913), which set out rules for fighting battles with <a href="/wiki/Toy_soldier" title="Toy soldier">toy soldiers</a> (miniatures).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toy_soldiers_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-toy_soldiers-78">[77]</a></sup> <i>Little Wars</i> is recognised today as the first <a href="/wiki/Miniature_wargaming" title="Miniature wargaming">recreational war game</a> and Wells is regarded by gamers and hobbyists as "the Father of Miniature War Gaming".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Pacificism" title="Pacificism">pacifist</a> prior to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, Wells stated "how much better is this amiable miniature [war] than the real thing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toy_soldiers_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-toy_soldiers-78">[77]</a></sup> According to Wells, the idea of the miniature war game developed from a visit by his friend <a href="/wiki/Jerome_K._Jerome" title="Jerome K. Jerome">Jerome K. Jerome</a>.  After dinner, Jerome began shooting down toy soldiers with a toy cannon and Wells joined in to compete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toy_soldiers_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-toy_soldiers-78">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travels_to_Russia">Travels to Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Travels to Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Wells visited Russia three times: 1914, 1920 and 1934. During his second visit, he saw his old friend <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky">Maxim Gorky</a> and with Gorky\'s help, met <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>. In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Russia_in_the_Shadows" title="Russia in the Shadows">Russia in the Shadows</a></i>, Wells portrayed Russia as recovering from a total social collapse, "the completest that has ever happened to any modern social organisation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> On 23 July 1934, after visiting U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, Wells went to the Soviet Union and interviewed <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> for three hours for the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> magazine, which was extremely rare at that time. He told Stalin how he had seen \'the happy faces of healthy people\' in contrast with his previous visit to Moscow in 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> However, he also criticised the lawlessness, class-based discrimination, state violence, and absence of free expression. Stalin enjoyed the conversation and replied accordingly. As the chairman of the London-based <a href="/wiki/PEN_International" title="PEN International">PEN Club</a>, which protected the rights of authors to write without being intimidated, Wells hoped by his trip to USSR, he could win Stalin over by force of argument. Before he left, he realized that no reform was to happen in the near future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Final years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg/170px-Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg/255px-Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg/340px-Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herbert_George_Wells_in_1943.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>H. G. Wells in 1943</div></div></div>\n<p>Wells\'s literary reputation declined as he spent his later years promoting causes that were rejected by most of his contemporaries as well as by younger authors whom he had previously influenced. In this connection, <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> described Wells as "too sane to understand the modern world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a> quipped: "Mr Wells is a born storyteller who has sold his birthright for a pot of message".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wells had <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> and was a co-founder in 1934 of The Diabetic Association (now <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_UK" title="Diabetes UK">Diabetes UK</a>, the leading charity for people with diabetes in the UK).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 October 1940, on the radio station <a href="/wiki/KTSA" title="KTSA">KTSA</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, Wells took part in a radio interview with <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a>, who two years previously had performed a famous <a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds_(radio_drama)" title="The War of the Worlds (radio drama)">radio adaptation of <i>The War of the Worlds</i></a>. During the interview, by Charles C Shaw, a KTSA radio host, Wells admitted his surprise at the widespread panic that resulted from the broadcast but acknowledged his debt to Welles for increasing sales of one of his "more obscure" titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells_(4643946137).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/H._G._Wells_%284643946137%29.jpg/170px-H._G._Wells_%284643946137%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/H._G._Wells_%284643946137%29.jpg/255px-H._G._Wells_%284643946137%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/H._G._Wells_%284643946137%29.jpg/340px-H._G._Wells_%284643946137%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H._G._Wells_(4643946137).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Commemorative <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> at Wells\' final home in <a href="/wiki/Regent%27s_Park" title="Regent\'s Park">Regent\'s Park</a>, London</div></div></div>\n<p>Wells died of unspecified causes on 13 August 1946, aged 79, at his home at 13 Hanover Terrace, overlooking <a href="/wiki/Regent%27s_Park" title="Regent\'s Park">Regent\'s Park</a>, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Some reports also say he died of a heart attack at the flat of a friend in London. In his preface to the 1941 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_in_the_Air" title="The War in the Air">The War in the Air</a></i>, Wells had stated that his <a href="/wiki/Epitaph" title="Epitaph">epitaph</a> should be: "I told you so. You <i>damned</i> fools".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Wells\' body was cremated at <a href="/wiki/Golders_Green_Crematorium" title="Golders Green Crematorium">Golders Green Crematorium</a> on 16 August 1946; his ashes were subsequently scattered into the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> at "<a href="/wiki/Old_Harry_Rocks" title="Old Harry Rocks">Old Harry Rocks</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A commemorative <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> in his honour was installed at his home in Regent\'s Park in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_views">Political views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Political views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Political_views_of_H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Political views of H. G. Wells (page does not exist)">Political views of H. G. Wells</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="616" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg/170px-Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg/255px-Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg/340px-Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Churchill_V_sign_HU_55521.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An avid reader of Wells\' books, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> wrote to the author in 1906, stating "I owe you a great debt", two days before giving an early landmark speech that the state should support its citizens, providing pensions, insurance and child welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Churchill_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill-94">[93]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Wells called his political views socialist. His contemporary political impact was limited, however, excluding his fiction\'s positivist stance on the leaps that could be made by <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> towards world peace. <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> was an avid reader of Wells\' books, and after they first met in 1902 they kept in touch until Wells died in 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Churchill_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill-94">[93]</a></sup> As a junior minister Churchill borrowed lines from Wells for one of his most famous early landmark speeches in 1906, and as Prime Minister the phrase "<a href="/wiki/The_Second_World_War_(book_series)" title="The Second World War (book series)">the gathering storm</a>" &#8212; used by Churchill to describe the rise of Nazi Germany &#8212; had been written by Wells in <i>The War of the Worlds</i>, which depicts an attack on Britain by Martians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Churchill_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill-94">[93]</a></sup> Wells\'s extensive writings on equality and human rights, most notably his most influential work, <i>The Rights of Man</i> (1940), laid the groundwork for the 1948 <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>, which was adopted by the United Nations shortly after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>His efforts regarding the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, on which he collaborated on the project with <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Woolf" title="Leonard Woolf">Leonard Woolf</a> with the booklets <i>The Idea of a League of Nations</i>, <i>Prolegomena to the Study of World Organization</i>, and <i>The Way of the League of Nations</i>,  became a disappointment as the organization turned out to be a weak one unable to prevent the Second World War, which itself occurred towards the very end of his life and only increased the pessimistic side of his nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> In his last book <i><a href="/wiki/Mind_at_the_End_of_Its_Tether" title="Mind at the End of Its Tether">Mind at the End of Its Tether</a></i> (1945), he considered the idea that humanity being replaced by another species might not be a bad idea. He referred to the era between the two World Wars as "The Age of Frustration".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>\nWells wrote in his book <i><a href="/wiki/God_the_Invisible_King" title="God the Invisible King">God the Invisible King</a></i> (1917) that his idea of God did not draw upon the traditional religions of the world: </p><blockquote><p>This book sets out as forcibly and exactly as possible the religious belief of the writer. [Which] is a profound belief in a personal and intimate God.&#160;... Putting the leading idea of this book very roughly, these two antagonistic typical conceptions of God may be best contrasted by speaking of one of them as God-as-Nature or the Creator, and of the other as God-as-Christ or the Redeemer. One is the great Outward God; the other is the Inmost God. The first idea was perhaps developed most highly and completely in the God of Spinoza. It is a conception of God tending to pantheism, to an idea of a comprehensive God as ruling with justice rather than affection, to a conception of aloofness and awestriking worshipfulness. The second idea, which is opposed to this idea of an absolute God, is the God of the human heart. The writer would suggest that the great outline of the theological struggles of that phase of civilisation and world unity which produced Christianity, was a persistent but unsuccessful attempt to get these two different ideas of God into one focus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> Later in the work, he aligns himself with a "renascent or modern religion&#160;... neither atheist nor Buddhist nor Mohammedan nor Christian&#160;... [that] he has found growing up in himself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, he said: "it is not now true for me.&#160;... Every believing Christian is, I am sure, my spiritual brother&#160;... but if systemically I called myself a Christian I feel that to most men I should imply too much and so tell a lie". Of other world religions, he writes: "All these religions are true for me as <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a> is a true thing and as a Swiss chalet is a true thing. There they are, and they have served a purpose, they have worked. Only they are not true for me to live in them.&#160;... They do not work for me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Fate_of_Homo_Sapiens" title="The Fate of Homo Sapiens">The Fate of Homo Sapiens</a></i> (1939), Wells criticised almost all world religions and philosophies, stating "there is no creed, no way of living left in the world at all, that really meets the needs of the time&#8230; When we come to look at them coolly and dispassionately, all the main religions, patriotic, moral and customary systems in which human beings are sheltering today, appear to be in a state of jostling and mutually destructive movement, like the houses and palaces and other buildings of some vast, sprawling city overtaken by a landslide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_influence">Literary influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Literary influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="785" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg/170px-H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg/255px-H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg/340px-H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_SWS_2906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>H. G. Wells as depicted in Gernsback\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_Wonder_Stories" title="Science Wonder Stories">Science Wonder Stories</a></i> in 1929</div></div></div>\n<p>The science fiction historian <a href="/wiki/John_Clute" title="John Clute">John Clute</a> describes Wells as "the most important writer the genre has yet seen", and notes his work has been central to both British and American science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jc_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-jc-103">[102]</a></sup> Science fiction author and critic <a href="/wiki/Algis_Budrys" title="Algis Budrys">Algis Budrys</a> said Wells "remains the outstanding expositor of both the hope, and the despair, which are embodied in the technology and which are the major facts of life in our world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-budrys196809_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-budrys196809-104">[103]</a></sup> He was nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> in 1921, 1932, 1935, and 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_nominations_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_nominations-10">[9]</a></sup> Wells so influenced real exploration of Mars that <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells_(crater)" title="H. G. Wells (crater)">an impact crater</a> on the planet was named after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn Britain, Wells\'s work was a key model for the British "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_Romance" title="Scientific Romance">Scientific Romance</a>", and other writers in that mode, such as <a href="/wiki/Olaf_Stapledon" title="Olaf Stapledon">Olaf Stapledon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._D._Beresford" title="J. D. Beresford">J. D. Beresford</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rb_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-rb-107">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/S._Fowler_Wright" title="S. Fowler Wright">S. Fowler Wright</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Mitchison" title="Naomi Mitchison">Naomi Mitchison</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> all drew on Wells\'s example. Wells was also an important influence on British science fiction of the period after the Second World War, with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Aldiss" title="Brian Aldiss">Brian Aldiss</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> expressing strong admiration for Wells\'s work.
