Among contemporary British science fiction writers, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Baxter_(author)" title="Stephen Baxter (author)">Stephen Baxter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Priest_(novelist)" title="Christopher Priest (novelist)">Christopher Priest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Roberts_(British_writer)" title="Adam Roberts (British writer)">Adam Roberts</a> have all acknowledged Wells\'s influence on their writing; all three are Vice-Presidents of the <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells_Society" title="H. G. Wells Society">H. G. Wells Society</a>. He also had a strong influence on British scientist <a href="/wiki/J._B._S._Haldane" title="J. B. S. Haldane">J. B. S. Haldane</a>, who wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Daedalus;_or,_Science_and_the_Future" title="Daedalus; or, Science and the Future">Daedalus; or, Science and the Future</a></i> (1924), "The Last Judgement" and "On Being the Right Size" from the essay collection <i>Possible Worlds</i> (1927), and <i>Biological Possibilities for the Human Species in the Next Ten Thousand Years</i> (1963), which are speculations about the future of human evolution and life on other planets. Haldane gave several lectures about these topics which in turn influenced other science fiction writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg/170px-Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg/255px-Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg/340px-Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_complete_science_adventure_books_1951win_n4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wells\'s works were reprinted in American science fiction magazines as late as the 1950s</div></div></div><p>In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Gernsback" title="Hugo Gernsback">Hugo Gernsback</a> reprinted most of Wells\'s work in the pulp magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Amazing_Stories" title="Amazing Stories">Amazing Stories</a></i>, regarding Wells\'s work as "texts of central importance to the self-conscious new genre".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jc_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-jc-103">[102]</a></sup> Later American writers such as <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Herbert" title="Frank Herbert">Frank Herbert</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jh2_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-jh2-117">[116]</a></sup> all recalled being influenced by Wells\'s work.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Lewis" title="Sinclair Lewis">Sinclair Lewis</a>\'s early novels were strongly influenced by Wells\'s realistic social novels, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Mr_Polly" title="The History of Mr Polly">The History of Mr Polly</a></i>; Lewis would also name his first son Wells after the author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Paris_Review" title="The Paris Review">The Paris Review</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a> described Wells as his favourite writer when he was a boy and "a great artist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nabokov_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nabokov-119">[118]</a></sup> He went on to cite <i>The Passionate Friends</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ann_Veronica" title="Ann Veronica">Ann Veronica</a></i>, <i>The Time Machine</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Country_of_the_Blind" title="The Country of the Blind">The Country of the Blind</a></i> as superior to anything else written by Wells\'s British contemporaries. In an apparent allusion to Wells\'s socialism and political themes, Nabokov said: "His sociological cogitations can be safely ignored, of course, but his romances and fantasies are superb."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nabokov_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nabokov-119">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="3921" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg/220px-Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg/330px-Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg/440px-Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herbert_George_Wells_Postal_stationery_envelope_Russia_2016_No_286.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2016 illustrated postal envelope with an image from <i>The War of the Worlds</i>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Post" title="Russian Post">Russian Post</a>, commemorating the 150th anniversary of the author\'s birth</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a> wrote many short pieces on Wells in which he demonstrates a deep familiarity with much of Wells\'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> While Borges wrote several critical reviews, including a mostly negative review of Wells\'s film <i>Things to Come</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> he regularly treated Wells as a canonical figure of fantastic literature. Late in his life, Borges included <i>The Invisible Man</i> and <i>The Time Machine</i> in his <i>Prologue to a Personal Library</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> a curated list of 100 great works of literature that he undertook at the behest of the Argentine publishing house <a href="/wiki/Emec%C3%A9_Editores" title="Emec&#233; Editores">Emec&#233;</a>. Canadian author <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a> read Wells\' books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moreau_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moreau-64">[63]</a></sup> and he also inspired writers of European speculative fiction such as <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel &#268;apek">Karel &#268;apek</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jh2_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-jh2-117">[116]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Yevgeny_Zamyatin" title="Yevgeny Zamyatin">Yevgeny Zamyatin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jh2_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-jh2-117">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Representations">Representations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Representations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary">Literary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Literary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>The superhuman protagonist of <a href="/wiki/J._D._Beresford" title="J. D. Beresford">J. D. Beresford</a>\'s 1911 novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hampdenshire_Wonder" title="The Hampdenshire Wonder">The Hampdenshire Wonder</a></i>, Victor Stott, was based on Wells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rb_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-rb-107">[106]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In <a href="/wiki/M._P._Shiel" title="M. P. Shiel">M. P. Shiel</a>\'s short story "The Primate of the Rose" (1928), there is an unpleasant womaniser named E. P. Crooks, who was written as a parody of Wells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arm_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-arm-123">[122]</a></sup> Wells had attacked Shiel\'s <i>Prince Zaleski</i> when it was published in 1895, and this was Shiel\'s response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arm_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-arm-123">[122]</a></sup> Wells praised Shiel\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Purple_Cloud" title="The Purple Cloud">The Purple Cloud</a></i> (1901); in turn Shiel expressed admiration for Wells, referring to him at a speech to the <a href="/wiki/Horsham" title="Horsham">Horsham</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotary_Club" title="Rotary Club">Rotary Club</a> in 1933 as "my friend Mr. Wells".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arm_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-arm-123">[122]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/That_Hideous_Strength" title="That Hideous Strength">That Hideous Strength</a></i> (1945), the character Jules is a caricature of Wells,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> and much of Lewis\'s science fiction was written both under the influence of Wells and as an antithesis to his work (or, as he put it, an "exorcism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> of the influence it had on him).</li>\n<li>In <a href="/wiki/Brian_Aldiss" title="Brian Aldiss">Brian Aldiss</a>\'s novella <i>The Saliva Tree</i> (1966), Wells has a small off screen guest role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In <a href="/wiki/Saul_Bellow" title="Saul Bellow">Saul Bellow</a>\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Sammler%27s_Planet" title="Mr. Sammler\'s Planet">Mr. Sammler\'s Planet</a></i> (1970), Wells is one of several historical figures the protagonist met when he was a young man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Map_of_Time" title="The Map of Time">The Map of Time</a></i> (2008) by Spanish author <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_J._Palma" title="F&#233;lix J. Palma">F&#233;lix J. Palma</a>; Wells is one of several historical characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Wells is one of the two Georges in <a href="/wiki/Paul_Levinson" title="Paul Levinson">Paul Levinson</a>\'s 2013 time-travel novelette, "Ian, George, and George," published in <i><a href="/wiki/Analog_Science_Fiction_and_Fact" title="Analog Science Fiction and Fact">Analog</a></i> magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dramatic">Dramatic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dramatic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Rod_Taylor" title="Rod Taylor">Rod Taylor</a> portrays Wells<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palumbo_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-palumbo-131">[130]</a></sup> in the 1960 <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">science fiction film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine_(1960_film)" title="The Time Machine (1960 film)">The Time Machine</a></i> (based on the novel of the same name), in which Wells uses his time machine to try and find his Utopian society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palumbo_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-palumbo-131">[130]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Malcolm_McDowell" title="Malcolm McDowell">Malcolm McDowell</a> portrays Wells in the 1979 science fiction film <i><a href="/wiki/Time_After_Time_(1979_film)" title="Time After Time (1979 film)">Time After Time</a></i>, in which Wells uses a time machine to pursue <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Ripper" title="Jack the Ripper">Jack the Ripper</a> to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palumbo_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-palumbo-131">[130]</a></sup> In the film, Wells meets "Amy" in the future who then returns to 1893 to become his second wife Amy Catherine Robbins.</li>\n<li>Wells is portrayed in the 1985 story <i><a href="/wiki/Timelash" title="Timelash">Timelash</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who_(season_22)" title="Doctor Who (season 22)">22nd season</a> of the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> science-fiction television series <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i>. In this story, Herbert, an enthusiastic temporary companion to the Doctor, is revealed to be a young H. G. Wells. The plot is loosely based upon the themes and characters of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i> with references to <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Invisible_Man" title="The Invisible Man">The Invisible Man</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau" title="The Island of Doctor Moreau">The Island of Doctor Moreau</a></i>. The story jokingly suggests that Wells\'s inspiration for his later novels came from his adventure with the <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Doctor" title="Sixth Doctor">Sixth Doctor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BBC2" title="BBC2">BBC2</a> anthology series <i>Encounters</i> about imagined meetings between historical figures, <i>Beautiful Lies</i>, by Paul Pender  (15 August 1992) centred on an acrimonious dinner party attended by Wells (<a href="/wiki/Richard_Todd" title="Richard Todd">Richard Todd</a>), <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jon_Finch" title="Jon Finch">Jon Finch</a>), and <a href="/wiki/William_Empson" title="William Empson">William Empson</a> (<a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ryecart" title="Patrick Ryecart">Patrick Ryecart</a>).</li>\n<li>The character of Wells also appeared in several episodes of <i><a href="/wiki/Lois_%26_Clark:_The_New_Adventures_of_Superman" title="Lois &amp; Clark: The New Adventures of Superman">Lois &amp; Clark: The New Adventures of Superman</a></i> (1993&#8211;1997), usually pitted against the time-travelling villain known as Tempus (<a href="/wiki/Lane_Davies" title="Lane Davies">Lane Davies</a>). Wells\'s younger self was played by <a href="/wiki/Terry_Kiser" title="Terry Kiser">Terry Kiser</a>, and the older Wells was played by <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Camp" title="Hamilton Camp">Hamilton Camp</a>.</li>\n<li>In the British TV mini-series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Infinite_Worlds_of_H._G._Wells" title="The Infinite Worlds of H. G. Wells">The Infinite Worlds of H. G. Wells</a></i> (2001), several of Wells\'s short stories are dramatised but are adapted using Wells himself (Tom Ward) as the main protagonist in each story.</li>\n<li>In the Disney Channel Original Series <i><a href="/wiki/Phil_of_the_Future" title="Phil of the Future">Phil of the Future</a></i>, which centres on time-travel, the present-day high school that the main characters attend is named "H. G. Wells".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In the 2006 television docudrama <i><a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells:_War_with_the_World" title="H. G. Wells: War with the World">H. G. Wells: War with the World</a></i>, Wells is played by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sheen" title="Michael Sheen">Michael Sheen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On the science fiction television series <i><a href="/wiki/Warehouse_13" title="Warehouse 13">Warehouse 13</a></i> (2009&#8211;2014), there is a female version Helena G. Wells. When she appeared she explained that her brother was her front for her writing because a female science fiction author would not be accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Comedian <a href="/wiki/Paul_F._Tompkins" title="Paul F. Tompkins">Paul F. Tompkins</a> portrays a fictional Wells as the host of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dead_Authors_Podcast" title="The Dead Authors Podcast">The Dead Authors Podcast</a></i>, wherein Wells uses his time machine to bring dead authors (played by other comedians) to the present and interview them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup></li>\n<li>H. G. Wells as a young boy appears in the <i><a href="/wiki/Legends_of_Tomorrow" title="Legends of Tomorrow">Legends of Tomorrow</a></i> episode "The Magnificent Eight". In this story, the boy Wells is dying of <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">consumption</a> but is cured by a time-travelling <a href="/wiki/Firestorm_(comics)" title="Firestorm (comics)">Martin Stein</a>.</li>\n<li>In the four part series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nightmare_Worlds_of_H._G._Wells" title="The Nightmare Worlds of H. G. Wells">The Nightmare Worlds of H. G. Wells</a></i> (2016), Wells is played by <a href="/wiki/Ray_Winstone" title="Ray Winstone">Ray Winstone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In the 2017 television series version of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_After_Time_(TV_series)" title="Time After Time (TV series)">Time After Time</a></i>, based on <a href="/wiki/Time_After_Time_(1979_film)" title="Time After Time (1979 film)">the 1979 film</a>, H. G. Wells is portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Stroma" title="Freddie Stroma">Freddie Stroma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_papers">Literary papers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Literary papers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1954, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana%E2%80%93Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana&#8211;Champaign">University of Illinois at Urbana&#8211;Champaign</a> purchased the H. G. Wells literary papers and correspondence collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-library.illinois.edu_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-library.illinois.edu-140">[139]</a></sup> The University\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Rare_Book_%26_Manuscript_Library_(University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign)" title="The Rare Book &amp; Manuscript Library (University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign)">Rare Book &amp; Manuscript Library</a> holds the largest collection of Wells manuscripts, correspondence, first editions and publications in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> Among these is an unpublished material and the manuscripts of such works as <i>The War of the Worlds</i> and <i>The Time Machine</i>. The collection includes first editions, revisions, translations. The letters contain general family correspondence, communications from publishers, material regarding the Fabian Society, and letters from politicians and public figures, most notably <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad">Joseph Conrad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-library.illinois.edu_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-library.illinois.edu-140">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells_bibliography" title="H. G. Wells bibliography">H. G. Wells bibliography</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-sfhof-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-sfhof_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Science fiction magazine editors <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Gernsback" title="Hugo Gernsback">Hugo Gernsback</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_W._Campbell" title="John W. Campbell">John W. Campbell</a> were the inaugural deceased members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy_Hall_of_Fame" title="Science Fiction and Fantasy Hall of Fame">Science Fiction and Fantasy Hall of Fame</a>, inducted in 1996 and followed annually by fiction writers Wells and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>, <a href="/wiki/C._L._Moore" title="C. L. Moore">C. L. Moore</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Heinlein" title="Robert Heinlein">Robert Heinlein</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abraham_Merritt" title="Abraham Merritt">Abraham Merritt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=H._G._Wells&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:H._G._Wells" title="Portal:H.
